<div id="review_901870087" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/901870087" />
    <a title="Bogdan" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1679953-bogdan"><img alt="Bogdan" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1364230014p2/1679953.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/901870087?book_show_action=true">Apr 05, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Bogdan" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Bogdan" href="/user/show/1679953-bogdan">Bogdan</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9789735027957" href="/book/show/17346106-ora-ul-i-c-inii">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1679953-bogdan?shelf=mundus-novus">mundus-novus</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer901870087" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7197703962559010338">A very complex novel, with multiple shifting perspectives, sometimes difficult to follow. But it also treats a very sensitive subject: violence, especially at teenagers. Set in a military school from Lima, it reminded me of the reasons I strongly opposed my father's idea when I finished high school, to join the Romanian Army. I also successfully escaped the mandatory military service, as it was abolished in 2007, although I was eligible couple of years before that moment.<br/><br/>The <b>premise</b>, as I explai</span>
  <span id="freeText7197703962559010338" style="display:none">A very complex novel, with multiple shifting perspectives, sometimes difficult to follow. But it also treats a very sensitive subject: violence, especially at teenagers. Set in a military school from Lima, it reminded me of the reasons I strongly opposed my father's idea when I finished high school, to join the Romanian Army. I also successfully escaped the mandatory military service, as it was abolished in 2007, although I was eligible couple of years before that moment.<br /><br />The <b>premise</b>, as I explained in my introduction, is about surviving in a violent world. Only the toughest are able to survive and adapt into such a world, while the ones that cannot turn into a brute able to apply physical correction to anyone are destined to dissapear. I believe this premise is admirably delivered and this is why I will give it a 5.<br /><br />Regarding the <b>form</b>, Vargas Llosa employed many shifting perspectives. At times, I was confused and I felt the need to note down what is the character from which perspective each part is written, then re-read the novel again. I did not do it, but still it was pretty close. This technique adds up a lot of depth to the narration and this is why I will give it a 4 for <b>form</b>.<br /><br />In terms of <b>originality</b>, I did not read before a similar book. The topic of violence appears in many other novels, but rarely is just this. Most of the time it just comes along with other important premise. I will give it a 5 at <b>originality</b>, as also the narrative technique employed was pretty new.<br /><br />The <b>characters</b> are all having unique voices and very well constructed. I believe it shows they may have been inspired by the people Mario has met in his experience in the same military school that is the setting of the novel. In such a situation, I will rate it with a 5.<br /><br />Regarding the <b>complexity and difficulty</b>, I admire the ability of the author to use so many distinctive narrative voices. On the other hand, the complexity generated by the numerous shifts, sometimes without a clear demarcation, makes it pretty hard to follow. So I will rate it with a 4 for <b>complexity and difficulty</b>.<br /><br />In terms of <b>credibility</b>, as this is inspired by the biographical events from the author's life, I hardly can debate it in terms of credibility. If there were fictionalized events, and I bet there were some, I cannot distinguish them from the others. This is why I will rate it with a 5.<br /><br />The last criteria is <b>edition</b>. I enjoyed this quality edition with good quality paper and cover. I hardly can find an issue with it. Due to this, I will rate it with a 5.<br /><br />To summarize, I liked it very much and it helped me recall some interesting things from my teenager years and youth. I strongly believe that there are places and times when the world can be like the one described in this novel, but thank God it is not at all like this for me. The physical violence shifted into other types of violence in most adult societies. All in all, my final rating for this collection is 4.71, which I will round to a 5 on Goodreads system.<br /><pre><br />+--------------------------+-----------------+<br />| Criteria | Rating |<br />+--------------------------+-----------------+<br />|Premise | 5 |<br />+--------------------------+-----------------+<br />|Form | 4 |<br />+--------------------------+-----------------+<br />|Originality | 5 |<br />+--------------------------+-----------------+<br />|Characters | 5 |<br />+--------------------------+-----------------+<br />|Difficulty/Complexity | 4 |<br />+--------------------------+-----------------+<br />|Credibility | 5 |<br />+--------------------------+-----------------+<br />|Edition | 5 |<br />+--------------------------+-----------------+<br />|Total | 4.71 |<br />+--------------------------+-----------------+<br /></pre><br />For more details on how I rated and reviewed this novel, please read these <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/story/show/353080-my-own-book-rating-system" rel="nofollow noopener">guidelines</a>.</span>
  <a data-text-id="7197703962559010338" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_2295765966" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2295765966" />
    <a title="Judy" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3333943-judy"><img alt="Judy" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1267037102p2/3333943.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2295765966?book_show_action=true">Feb 19, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Judy" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Judy" href="/user/show/3333943-judy">Judy</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780571173204" href="/book/show/119945.The_Time_of_the_Hero">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3333943-judy?shelf=literature-in-translation">literature-in-translation</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3333943-judy?shelf=20th-century-fiction">20th-century-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3333943-judy?shelf=books-from-1963">books-from-1963</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3333943-judy?shelf=nobel-prize-author">nobel-prize-author</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2295765966" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14877604505520764372"> I read my first book by this author in 2002. <i>The Green House</i>, his second novel, was one of the challenging literary novels I was starting to read in those years, having somewhat satisfied my desire for trashy, escape reading. I was rekindling my aspirations as a writer and wanting to read Literature. It was around that time that I invented My Big Fat Reading Project. Vargas Llosa was one of the celebrated authors of the Latin American boom and went on to win the Nobel Prize in 2010.<br/> <br/><i>The Time of</i></span>
  <span id="freeText14877604505520764372" style="display:none"> I read my first book by this author in 2002. <i>The Green House</i>, his second novel, was one of the challenging literary novels I was starting to read in those years, having somewhat satisfied my desire for trashy, escape reading. I was rekindling my aspirations as a writer and wanting to read Literature. It was around that time that I invented My Big Fat Reading Project. Vargas Llosa was one of the celebrated authors of the Latin American boom and went on to win the Nobel Prize in 2010.<br /> <br /><i>The Time of the Hero</i> is his first novel and also a challenge to read: multiple characters, a disjointed time sequence, switches from third to first person and back again. Often I was not sure who was who, especially since many characters had real names and nicknames. OK. Enough whining. I did catch on and was spellbound by the end.<br /><br />Most of the book takes place behind the walls of the Leoncio Prado Military Academy on the edge of Lima, Peru. It follows a group of cadets through their four years there, the equivalent of high school. The boys come from different socioeconomic backgrounds and were sent there for various reasons but the underlying intention is to produce soldiers. Peru has a long history of war and unrest.<br /><br />The titular "hero" was placed in the Academy by his mostly absent, philandering father for the purpose of "making a man of him" and steering him away from the suspected unmanliness of his poetry writing. (Vargas Llosa himself was sent to a military academy by his father for similar reasons. Just goes to show you.)<br /><br />About eight of these boys stay together as a sort of gang for the duration. They give each other nicknames like The Jaguar, The Boa, Curly, The Slave, and The Poet. The Jaguar is their leader, a tough sociopath who is the best at never getting caught and never giving a fuck about anyone. They drink and smoke, they joke constantly about sex, they have sex with each other (consensual and predatory), all without their teachers and officers catching on.<br /><br />One night this rowdy group of pubescent male creatures draw straws and send the loser to steal a chemistry exam from the school building. Good grades are one of the conditions of getting leave. Another cadet informs on the guy and is ultimately murdered by one of the boys.<br /><br />What a gnarly tale! Along the way the back stories and family lives of several characters are revealed including their romantic/lustful encounters with young girls in Lima. The authorities conduct an investigation after the murder and all comes to light but they decide on a cover up to protect the reputation of the Academy, the officers, and the military in general. This includes throwing the most principled and honest officer under the bus.<br /><br />The novel is a morality tale, a mystery, and a treatise on what constitutes heroism. In the end, the murderer is revealed to the reader as is the fate of The Poet. It is only then that it becomes clear which back story goes with which cadet. I had not seen the denouement coming, the sign of a good novel.<br /><br />I was left somewhat in awe of how Vargas Llosa constructed such a novel for his first time out. It is perhaps a bit overdone, a bit pretentiously obfuscating, but it shows deep thinking about Peru, life, and big ideas like good, evil, honor, truth, and love.<br /><br />This quote from Jean Paul Sartre, the epigraph before Part One, sums it up well:<br />"We play the part of heroes because we're cowards, the part of saints because we're wicked: we play the killer's role because we're dying to murder our fellow man: we play at being because we're liars from the moment we're born."</span>
  <a data-text-id="14877604505520764372" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">

<span id="freeTextContainer13800171580574439581">A very compelling read, to say the least. But with a bit of a confusing narrative style. Many people have compared this to lord of the flies, but I have yet to read that book. Safe to say, this is how I imagine it would be.<br/><br/>The descriptions are vivid, and as a teen who once studied far away from home, I can tell that more than half of what's described in the book might have, in one capacity or another, actually happened.   <span id="freeText13800171580574439581" style="display:none">A very compelling read, to say the least. But with a bit of a confusing narrative style. Many people have compared this to lord of the flies, but I have yet to read that book. Safe to say, this is how I imagine it would be.<br /><br />The descriptions are vivid, and as a teen who once studied far away from home, I can tell that more than half of what's described in the book might have, in one capacity or another, actually happened. And I'm pretty sure of it, despite the school I attended not being a Military School. <br /><br />All in all, a very solid read, and starts picking up pace after part two. Hard to believe this was only his debut book, because it is quite good. I have quite a few Vargas Llosa books lined up for reading, and this was only my second one, after Feast of the Goat. Needless to say he is slowly becoming one of my favorite authors. <br /><br />Also left me yearning for cigarettes more than once throughout the book.</span>

<div id="review_703201326" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/703201326" />
    <a title="John Gurney" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/19356945-john-gurney"><img alt="John Gurney" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1365779791p2/19356945.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/703201326?book_show_action=true">Aug 24, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="John Gurney" class="user" itemprop="url" name="John Gurney" href="/user/show/19356945-john-gurney">John Gurney</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780374520212" href="/book/show/1293708.The_Time_of_the_Hero">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer703201326" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1891714718318716492">This coming-of-age tale was so realistic - and its portrayal of the Leoncio Prado Military Academy so unflattering - the Peruvian Army bought up every available copy to burn the books. <br/><br/>Vargas-Llosa's main character, Alberto, the jaded "Poet" cadet, is relatable and multi-faceted. Other characters, especially the slippery cadet nicknamed the Jaguar, are realistic and intriguing. The Nobel Prize-winning author's descriptions are vivid, the setting of Lima is unique, and his plot is multi-dimension</span>
  <span id="freeText1891714718318716492" style="display:none">This coming-of-age tale was so realistic - and its portrayal of the Leoncio Prado Military Academy so unflattering - the Peruvian Army bought up every available copy to burn the books. <br /><br />Vargas-Llosa's main character, Alberto, the jaded "Poet" cadet, is relatable and multi-faceted. Other characters, especially the slippery cadet nicknamed the Jaguar, are realistic and intriguing. The Nobel Prize-winning author's descriptions are vivid, the setting of Lima is unique, and his plot is multi-dimensional and suspenseful. The story explores adolescent love lost, familial problems, petty crime and murder. A group of same year cadets, loyal to each other, fight an often hypocritical army bureaucracy in every way. How far will loyalty go when the criminal stakes are raised?<br /><br />Whereas many Latin American writers of the same period toyed with magical realism and far-fetched, even bizarre, plots, everything about Time of the Hero is realistic, even gritty. It is written in a third-person POV, except for a few first-person vignettes from a mystery character, not revealed until the end. <br /><br />This was Vargas-Llosa's first novel, and its first small run, partly depleted by book-burning soldiers, was followed by many new editions and foreign translations. If you're new to Vargas-Llosa, I'd recommend either this book or Feast of the Goat. Better yet, read them both.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="1891714718318716492" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_665969040" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/665969040" />
    <a title="Jeremy Allan" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/657496-jeremy-allan"><img alt="Jeremy Allan" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1299236455p2/657496.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/665969040?book_show_action=true">Jul 12, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jeremy Allan" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jeremy Allan" href="/user/show/657496-jeremy-allan">Jeremy Allan</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780374520212" href="/book/show/1293708.The_Time_of_the_Hero">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/657496-jeremy-allan?shelf=modern-and-contemporary-fiction">modern-and-contemporary-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer665969040" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17224136997481055571">Mario Vargas Llosa's first book is dark, often times deliberately elusive, and surprising. It revolves around a group of teenage cadets, struggling to survive a hellish military education, and grappling with each other as a means of coping with many misdirected emotions. They negotiate uneasy alliances and wade through institutionalized violence. Through shifts in perspective, a range of narrative techniques, and sensible but wild changes of emotion in the characters, Vargas Llosa provides us wi</span>
  <span id="freeText17224136997481055571" style="display:none">Mario Vargas Llosa's first book is dark, often times deliberately elusive, and surprising. It revolves around a group of teenage cadets, struggling to survive a hellish military education, and grappling with each other as a means of coping with many misdirected emotions. They negotiate uneasy alliances and wade through institutionalized violence. Through shifts in perspective, a range of narrative techniques, and sensible but wild changes of emotion in the characters, Vargas Llosa provides us with an evocative book. It is horribly masculine in its perspective, though, even if that might not be a surprise regarding a book that revolves around a boys' military academy. There is only one truly important female character, and she is passive to the limits of what is believable. In a sense, this is a part of the culture from which this book springs. There is even a way to read it as a critique of this kind of hyper-attenuated male world, with all its violence, confusion about homoerotic desires, and dark currents of taboo. The outcome, though, is that however we read it, it doesn't provide us with a world we'd like to inhabit any longer than the pages ask us to, if for that long even. I enjoyed this novel very much, and I see its mastery, but I'm also glad to have finished it.</span>
  <a data-text-id="17224136997481055571" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">

  <div class="friendReviews elementListBrown" >
    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_667636876" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/667636876" />
    <a title="Andrew" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/45836-andrew"><img alt="Andrew" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1421786395p2/45836.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/667636876?book_show_action=true">Jul 14, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Andrew" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Andrew" href="/user/show/45836-andrew">Andrew</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9788420412337" href="/book/show/15919922-la-ciudad-y-los-perros">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/45836-andrew?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/45836-andrew?shelf=literature-modern">literature-modern</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer667636876" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2391731757841559195">(Crítica en español primero. . . <u>English review below</u>)<br/><br/>Otro libro que ocupa espacio simultaneamente en los estantes de "clásico" y "moderno," esto es por lejos la novela más difícil que nunca he leído en español. Tan difícil que por primera vez pienso que me hubiese gustado significativamente más leerla en mi inglés nativo. Tan difícil que me aburría cuando ni podía entender quien estaba narrando por gran parte del libro (y me alivió muchísimo en fin cuando me enteré que ¡me suponía sentir así!).</span>
  <span id="freeText2391731757841559195" style="display:none">(Crítica en español primero. . . <u>English review below</u>)<br /><br />Otro libro que ocupa espacio simultaneamente en los estantes de "clásico" y "moderno," esto es por lejos la novela más difícil que nunca he leído en español. Tan difícil que por primera vez pienso que me hubiese gustado significativamente más leerla en mi inglés nativo. Tan difícil que me aburría cuando ni podía entender quien estaba narrando por gran parte del libro (y me alivió muchísimo en fin cuando me enteré que ¡me suponía sentir así!). Tan difícil que iba a calificarla con tres solas estrellas hasta que leyera las magníficas críticas incluidos en mi edición que me explicaron el impresionante genio tras la forma y los temas. <br /><br />Pero wow, ahora lo considero como no solo un libro emblemático de Vargas Llosa, de Peru y de Latino America, pero emblemático de toda la literatura moderna. Y los analices acompañantes -- especialmente los de Victor García de la Concha y de Javier Cercas -- me convencieron de algo mucho más impresionante de la calidad del libro y la destreza con cual lo fabricó Vargas Llosa. Me convencieron por primera vez de la importancia de la literatura moderna. . . la importancia no -- sino <i>la necesidad</i>. Y después de autores como Flaubert, Faulkner, Cortázar y Borges, Vargas Llosa era en la vanguardia de ese movimiento, lo cual me causa mucha admiración para el maestro peruano.<br /><br />Lectores de mis críticas -- especialmente los recientes -- me conocen como una persona sin paciencia por los experimentos puros de forma. Yo requiero historias encima de todo en la ficción que leo. Puedo apreciar técnicas novedosas e interesantes, pero sin una buena historia me fastidian y me parecen una pérdida de tiempo. Pero ¿qué voy a decir yo cuando encuentro un comentario así de cierto?:<blockquote>Vargas Llosa piensa que la novela tiene que contar una buena historia pero, a diferencia de muchos novelistas ingenuos de nuestra época. . . Vargas Llosa no ignora que la novela es forma, y que por tanto la bondad de la historia que cuenta depende de la forma en que está contada: <i><b>una mala historia bien contada es una buena historia, mientras que una buena historia mal contada es una mala historia</b></i> (p.478-9, énfasis mía).</blockquote>¿Cómo se puede discutir con ese sentimiento? Y si no se puede discutir, hay que admitir que la <i>forma</i> puede superar en importancia a la propia <i>historia</i>, algo que yo he esencialmente negado por toda la vida. Y si se admite eso, hay que afirmar la importancia de la literatura moderna en comparación con la clásica, la cual casi nunca enfrentó al problema del narrador. Es algo que nunca creía. Es decir, este libro es algo revolucionario para mi, y me pasma tanto expresarlo así porque ni disfruté tanto de la propia novela mientras leía. <br /><br />La historia es bastante sencilla -- un poco de <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/7624.Lord_of_the_Flies" title="Lord of the Flies by William Golding" rel="nofollow noopener">Lord of the Flies</a> -- y aún decepcionante en que no cumpla con las expectativas tradicionales; nunca se sabe la verdad de la chocante muerte que ocurre en la mera mitad del libro. Pero la estructura y los temas poderosos son los que tiene este libro de especial. La perspectiva cambia principalmente entre cuatro personas: el débil Esclavo, el cínico Poeta, el perverso Boa y el peligroso Jaguar. A veces hay narrador objetivo para comunicar eventos más grandes, pero por lo general estás experimentando la vida de colegio militar (o cotidiana en la ciudad de Lima) a través de los ojos de un adolescente.<br /><br />Como ya mencioné, por la mayoría del libro ni sabes la identidad de uno de los narradores (y no voy a arruinar la sorpresa aquí). Y los cambios de perspectiva y de época son confusos y desagradables en el principio -- interrumpen cualquier progreso que estás haciendo por coger el ritmo de la historia. Pero si puedes leer con atención y paciencia, hay gran premio en el final cuando entiendas la meta y te des cuenta de la gran realización que hizo Vargas Llosa. Literalmente cambia de protagonista en el mero final, y ¡resulta buenísimo! Y por parte de las críticas se entera que esta idea de retar al lector es algo esencial de la literature moderna. Es un estilo que requiere casi tanta atención como la poesía, algo que la verdad no me conviene mucho. Pero como dije, un libro así de genial me podría convertir.<br /><br />Además, me encantaron los trayectos de los personajes, dos de tragedia y los otros dos más optimistas. La cuestión de lo moral en todos los acontecimientos es bien interesante, y los personajes que parecen morales en el principio no son los que parecen en el final (y vice versa). También fascinante es el comentario social a través de cada personaje -- cada cual vive la realidad de su estrato social, pero otra vez de manera sorprendente. Y en esa manera también es un libro sumamente limeño (o peruano); aquí Vargas Llosa hace por Lima lo que Garcia Márquez hizo para la costa Colombiana con <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/24424587.Cien_a_os_de_soledad" title="Cien años de soledad by Gabriel García Márquez" rel="nofollow noopener">Cien años de soledad</a>, o lo que Juan Gabriel Vásquez hizo para Bogotá con <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/11363624.El_ruido_de_las_cosas_al_caer" title="El ruido de las cosas al caer by Juan Gabriel Vásquez" rel="nofollow noopener">El ruido de las cosas al caer</a>, lo que acabo de leer (aquí está <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1133952281" rel="nofollow noopener">mi crítica</a>). Cada cual permite que un extranjero comprenda su hogar lo más pleno posible sin visitarlo.<br /><br />En fin, necesito leerlo de nuevo. No es perfecto -- todavía me parece un poco largo, y tal vez innecesariamente sin rítmo -- pero es una obra bien impresionante, y aún revolucionario (no solo para mi, pero en general también). No lo considero tan encantador como las únicas otras novelas de Vargas Llosa que he leido -- <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/53925.The_War_of_the_End_of_the_World" title="The War of the End of the World by Mario Vargas Llosa" rel="nofollow noopener">The War of the End of the World</a> y <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/53969.The_Feast_of_the_Goat" title="The Feast of the Goat by Mario Vargas Llosa" rel="nofollow noopener">The Feast of the Goat</a> -- pero sí es mucho más importante. A diferencia de otros criticos, no lo recomiendo a los novatos de Vargas Llosa -- es muy complejo para eso. Pero a cualquiera que le haya gustado un libro de él, sí es necesario leer esto. Los analices acompañantes son también geniales y los recomiendo fuertemente casi todos. Estoy muy agradecido de haberlo leido a pesar de no disfrutar tanto de la experiencia mientras lo hice. <br /><br />Para mi es y siempre será una marca en mi descubrimiento de la literatura moderna, y ojalá que marque una nueva etapa en mi conciencia de lector. Por tanto tiempo me he preguntado ¿Qué de los modernos estoy perdiendo? Pero ahora lo comprendo, gracias a Sr. Vargas Llosa y sus varios críticos.<br /><br /><br />------------------------------<br /><br />Another book that occupies space on both the "classic" and "modern" shelves, this is by far the most difficult novel I've ever read in Spanish. So difficult that for the first time I think I would have liked it significantly more had I read it in my native English. So difficult that it irritated and bored me when I couldn't even understand who was narrating for a large part of the book (though I eventually found out to my great relief that I was supposed to feel that way!). So difficult that I was going to give it three measly stars until I read the magnificent essays included in my edition which explained the amazing genius behind the structure and theme.<br /><br />But wow, now I consider it not only a landmark work for Vargas Llosa, for Peru and for all of Latin America, but also a landmark of modern literature period. And the accompanying analyses -- especially those by Victor García de la Concha and Javier Cercas -- convinced me of something much more impressive than the novel's quality and Vargas Llosa's skill in bringing it to fruition; they convinced me for the first time of the importance of modern literature. . . the importance no, but rather <i>the need</i> for it. And after authors like Flaubert, Faulkner, Cortázar and Borges, Vargas Llosa was at the forefront of this modern movement, a fact causing me endless admiration for the Peruvian master. <br /><br />Readers of my reviews -- especially my recent ones -- know me as someone without much patience for pure experiments in form. I need <i>story</i> above all in my fiction. I can appreciate novel and interesting techniques, but without a substantive story they annoy me and seem a waste of time. But what can I say when I encounter such an indisputable truth as this (my translation):<blockquote>Vargas Llosa thinks that a novel must tell a good story, but contrary to many naive novelists of our era. . . Vargas Llosa does not ignore that the novel is form and that as such the quality of the story that it tells depends on the form in which it is told: <i><b> a bad story well told is a good story, while a good story poorly told is a bad story</b></i> (p. 478-9, my emphasis).</blockquote>How can you really argue with the truth of that statement? And if you can't argue it, you must admit that <i>form</i> could exceed in importance even <i>story</i>, something that I have essentially denied my entire life. And if you admit that, you have to affirm the importance of modern literature when compared to the classics which left the narrator question almost entirely unasked. This is something I never believed before. Which is to say, this book has been truly revolutionary for me, amazing since I didn't even really enjoy it while reading.<br /><br />The story is fairly simple -- vaguely Lord of the Flies-ish -- and even off-putting in that it doesn't meet traditional expectations; you never discover the truth of the shocking death that occurs halfway through. But the structure and powerful themes are the special parts of the book. The perspective changes principally between four people: the weak Slave, the cynical Poet, the twisted Boa and the dangerous Jaguar. At times an objective narrator conveys broader events, but generally you are experiencing life at a military school (or daily life in the city of Lima) through the eyes of an adolescent.<br /><br />As I mentioned, for the majority of the book you don't even know the identity of one of the above narrators (I won't ruin it by saying who). And the changes of perspective and chronology are confusing and off-putting throughout -- they interrupt whatever small progress you're making to pick up the rhythm of the story. But if you can read with attention and patience, there's a great reward at the end when you understand the ultimate aim (therefore comprehending Vargas Llosa's great achievement). He literally switches the protagonist out from under you at the very end, and it works! And from the essays you find out that this idea of challenging the reader is essential to modern literature. It's a style that requires almost as much attention as poetry, something that I'm personally not given to myself. But as I said, a novel like this might just change me for the better.<br /><br />Moreover, I loved the character arcs, two of which are tragic and two more optimistic. The moral question in all of this is fascinating, and the characters that seem the most moral in the beginning are not those that seem so at the end (and vice versa). Also fascinating is the social commentary through each character -- each one literally lives the reality of their entire social class, for better or worse, and in a way that surprises. And in that way too the book is purely Lima (or Peru); Vargas Llosa does for his city and country what Garcia Márquez did for the Colombian coast with <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/320.One_Hundred_Years_of_Solitude" title="One Hundred Years of Solitude by Gabriel García Márquez" rel="nofollow noopener">One Hundred Years of Solitude</a>, or what Juan Gabriel Vásquez did for Bogotá with <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/16171259.The_Sound_of_Things_Falling" title="The Sound of Things Falling by Juan Gabriel Vásquez" rel="nofollow noopener">The Sound of Things Falling</a>, which I just read (here's <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1133952281" rel="nofollow noopener">my review</a>). Each one permits an outsider to see their home in the strongest way possible without physically visiting.<br /><br />To sum up, I need to read this again. It's not perfect -- it still seems a little long and perhaps needlessly arhythmic -- but it's a masterpiece, even revolutionary (and not just for me, also in general). It's not as enjoyable as either of the two other novels I've read by Vargas Llosa -- <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/53925.The_War_of_the_End_of_the_World" title="The War of the End of the World by Mario Vargas Llosa" rel="nofollow noopener">The War of the End of the World</a> or <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/53969.The_Feast_of_the_Goat" title="The Feast of the Goat by Mario Vargas Llosa" rel="nofollow noopener">The Feast of the Goat</a> -- but it's more consequential by far. Unlike other reviewers I would not recommend it to Vargas Llosa virgins as I think it's too complex, but anyone who has enjoyed a novel of his should read this immediately. The accompanying literary criticisms are also highly recommended. I'm very grateful to have read it despite not fully enjoying the experience as I was doing it. <br /><br />For me it is and will always be a landmark in my discovery of modern literature, and hopefully the mark of a new era in my reading awareness. For so long I've been wondering what it is about modern literature that I just don't get. Well, now I get it, thanks to Sr. Vargas Llosa and all of his interpreters.<br /><br /><a href="http://notbadmoviereviews.wordpress.com/books/ciudad-y-perros/" rel="nofollow noopener">Not Bad Reviews</a><br /><br /><a href="http://www.twitter.com/blakerosser1" rel="nofollow noopener">@blakerosser1</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="2391731757841559195" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_2429337987" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2429337987" />
    <a title="Sam" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1954710-sam"><img alt="Sam" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1489370002p2/1954710.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2429337987?book_show_action=true">Jul 03, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sam" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sam" href="/user/show/1954710-sam">Sam</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2429337987" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16399015435338395570">This book was very hard to put down. It’s a Lord of the Flies-style novel about how cruel young men can be to one another. The military school Leoncio Prado is a microcosm of Lima, or Peru, itself. There is serious prejudice, and self-preservation is the name of the game. <br/><br/>After reading a couple of other, later novels from Vargas Llosa (Tía Julia and La casa verde), I thought I was done with him. But this is way better than those other two. I think the main thing is that those other novels had fe</span>
  <span id="freeText16399015435338395570" style="display:none">This book was very hard to put down. It’s a Lord of the Flies-style novel about how cruel young men can be to one another. The military school Leoncio Prado is a microcosm of Lima, or Peru, itself. There is serious prejudice, and self-preservation is the name of the game. <br /><br />After reading a couple of other, later novels from Vargas Llosa (Tía Julia and La casa verde), I thought I was done with him. But this is way better than those other two. I think the main thing is that those other novels had female characters that, in my opinion, Vargas Llosa didn’t write well. In La ciudad y los perros, there really aren’t any central females. They are side characters, and he is way better at writing male characters. <br /><br />The jumpy narration takes some getting used to (in the forward, Vargas Llosa acknowledges how much he was reading Faulkner at the time), but you really get an in-depth portrayal of some of these characters. It’s worth persevering through the initial confusion, and the twists are quite well done. <br /><br />This is one of those famous novels that does not disappoint, in spite of the burden of that fame, 50 years after it was published. Is it better than A Hundred Years of Solitude? That’s one of those questions for the ages, and this is one of those few books that can stand next to GGM’s 1967 masterpiece. Even though it is a bit long, read this if you are considering diving into the literary world of Mario Vargas Llosa. </span>
  <a data-text-id="16399015435338395570" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2426879444?book_show_action=true">Jun 19, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ken" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ken" href="/user/show/38999260-ken">Ken</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2426879444" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8445397644355859200">Set in a military camp in Lima, Peru. This story details the lives of the young cadets as they weave through the confinement, discipline and boredom at the camp.<br/>A series of pranks that soon spiral out of control that leads to a death on the camp, this event could lead to jeopardising the entire military hierarchy.<br/><br/>You can tell Llosa is writing from he’s own personal experiences as the day to day life is perfectly described.<br/>My only criticism would be that the story switches between first and third</span>
  <span id="freeText8445397644355859200" style="display:none">Set in a military camp in Lima, Peru. This story details the lives of the young cadets as they weave through the confinement, discipline and boredom at the camp.<br />A series of pranks that soon spiral out of control that leads to a death on the camp, this event could lead to jeopardising the entire military hierarchy.<br /><br />You can tell Llosa is writing from he’s own personal experiences as the day to day life is perfectly described.<br />My only criticism would be that the story switches between first and third person narration, which took me a while to adjust.<br />But the well written prose soon drew me back in.</span>

<div class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground">Readers also enjoyed</h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent"><div class='bookCarousel'>
<div class='carouselRow' style='width: 3600px'>

//<![CDATA[  

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('14BWOCeI1Y8KQGr+omMuIUczPT3h/Nbc5FsghKpKCb1xQjCjDyOVrUApsQVNHtBtG1aoE+imUWSAgVJOraLKOA==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('qBqsXs2BUWaeHrlCVm3nRbOIxMUhMXayiKojISFVF70O2MrF5SoRRNR3Yrm5EBkJ7+1R6yhr8QrscFHrJr3UOA==')})
  }
//]]>
</script>

Para quien todavía no la leyó, El túnel es la mejor introducción al universo prodigioso de Ernesto Sábato; para quien la conoce, un clásico de las letras del continente, una historia sobre el drama del hombre arrojado en el sinsentido más doloroso: la conciencia de la nada. \nEl narrador describe una historia de amor y muerte en la que muestra la soledad del individuo contemporáneo. No están ausentes de esta trama policial y de suspenso, la locura y la increíble reflexión del protagonista, el pintor Juan Pablo Castel, debatiéndose por comprender las causas que lo arrastraron a matar a la mujer que amaba, María Iribarne, y que era su única vía de salvación. En este alucinante drama de la vida interior, seres intrincados en la bestial búsqueda de comprensión ceden a la mentira, la hipocresía y los celos desmedidos hasta el crimen más inexplicable. Aventura amorosa, aventura onírica, aventura del ser que dan testimonio de un asesinato, de cierta memoria culpable y de una valiente introspección. \nTécnicamente perfecta y de lectura apasionante, El túnel excede el negativismo ácido de Sartre y la frenética huida hacia el vacío que plantea El extranjero de Camus, pero tiene de esos dos maestros literarios la impronta genial que hace de la He saw the beauty of the Peruvian landscape, as well as the grimness of social conditions in the Andes, through the eyes of the Indians who are a part of it. Ernesto, the narrator of Deep Rivers, is a child with origins in two worlds. The son of a wandering country lawyer, he is brought up by Indian servants until he enters a Catholic boarding school at age 14. In this urban Spanish environment he is a misfit and a loner. The conflict of the Indian and the Spanish cultures is acted out within him as it was in the life of Arguedas. For the boy Ernesto, salvation is his world of dreams and memories. While Arguedas\' poetry was published in Quechua, he invented a language for his novels in which he used native syntax with Spanish vocabulary. This makes translation into other languages extremely difficult, and Frances Horning Barraclough has done a masterful job, winning the 1978 Translation Center Award from Columbia University for her       var newTip = new Tip($('bookCover_53413'), "\n\n  <h2><a class=\"readable bookTitle\" href=\"https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/53413.Hopscotch?from_choice=false&amp;from_home_module=false\">Hopscotch<\/a><\/h2>\n\n      <div>\n        by <a class=\"authorName\" href=\"/author/show/25824.Julio_Cort_zar?a=9&amp;origin=related_works\">Julio Cortázar<\/a>\n      <\/div>\n\n          <div class=\"smallText uitext darkGreyText\">\n            <span class=\"minirating\"><span class=\"stars staticStars notranslate\"><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p3\"><\/span><\/span> 4.23 avg rating &mdash; 30,966 ratings<\/span>            &mdash; published 1963\n          <\/div>\n\n    <div class=\"addBookTipDescription\">\n      \n<span id=\"freeTextContainer17318049513970247903\">Horacio Oliveira is an Argentinian writer who lives in Paris with his mistress, La Maga, surrounded by a loose-knit circle of bohemian friends who call themselves \"the Club.\" A child\'s death and La Maga\'s disappearance put an end to his life of empty<\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText17318049513970247903\" style=\"display:none\">Horacio Oliveira is an Argentinian writer who lives in Paris with his mistress, La Maga, surrounded by a loose-knit circle of bohemian friends who call themselves &quot;the Club.&quot; A child\'s death and La Maga\'s disappearance put an end to his life of empty pleasures and intellectual acrobatics, and prompt Oliveira to return to Buenos Aires, where he works by turns as a salesman, a keeper of a circus cat which can truly count, and an attendant in an insane asylum. Hopscotch is the dazzling, freewheeling account of Oliveira\'s astonishing adventures.\n\nThe book is highly influenced by Henry Miller’s reckless and relentless search for truth in post-decadent Paris and Daisetz Teitaro Suzuki’s modal teachings on Zen Buddhism.\n\nCortázar\'s employment of interior monologue, punning, slang, and his use of different languages is reminiscent of Modernist writers like Joyce, although his main influences were Surrealism and the French New Novel, as well as the &quot;riffing&quot; aesthetic of jazz and New Wave Cinema.\n\nIn 1966, Gregory Rabassa won the first National Book Award to recognize the work of a translator, for his English-language edition of Hopscotch. Julio Cortázar was so pleased with Rabassa\'s translation of Hopscotch that he recommended the translator to Gabriel García Márquez when García Márquez was looking for someone to translate his novel One Hundred Years of Solitude into English. &quot;Rabassa\'s One Hundred Years of Solitude improved the original,&quot; according to García Márquez.<\/span>\n  <a data-text-id=\"17318049513970247903\" A violent showdown in the Sonora desert turns search to flight; twenty years later Belano and Lima are still on the run.\n\nThe explosive first long work by “the most exciting writer to come from south of the Rio Grande in a long time” (Ilan Stavans, Los Angeles Times), The Savage Detectives follows Belano and Lima through the eyes of the people whose paths they cross in Central America, Europe, Israel, and West Africa. This chorus includes the muses of visceral realism, the beautiful Font sisters; their father, an architect interned in a Mexico City asylum; a sensitive young follower of Octavio Paz; a foul-mouthed American graduate student; a French girl with a taste for the Marquis de Sade; the great-granddaughter of Leon Trotsky; a Chilean stowaway with a mystical gift for numbers; the anorexic heiress to a Mexican underwear empire; an Argentinian photojournalist in Angola; and assorted hangers-on, detractors, critics, lovers, employers, vagabonds, real-life literary figures, and random acquaintances.\n\nA polymathic descendant of Borges and Pynchon, Roberto Bolaño traces the hidden connection between literature and violence in a world where national boundaries are fluid and death lurks in the shadow of the avant-garde.       <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/22515.Mario_Vargas_Llosa">About Mario Vargas Llosa</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/22515.Mario_Vargas_Llosa"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1569483424p3/22515.jpg);'></div>
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/22515.Mario_Vargas_Llosa">Mario Vargas Llosa
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__followerCount'>
6,205
followers
</div>

</div>
</div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer15721658988519803355">Mario Vargas Llosa, born in Peru in 1936, is the author of some of the most significant writing to come out of South America in the past fifty years. His novels include <i>The Green House</i>, about a brothel in a Peruvian town that brings together the innocent and the corrupt; <i>The Feast of the Goat</i>, a vivid re-creation of the Dominican Republic during the final days of General Rafael Trujillo’s insidiou</span>
  <span id="freeText15721658988519803355" style="display:none">Mario Vargas Llosa, born in Peru in 1936, is the author of some of the most significant writing to come out of South America in the past fifty years. His novels include <i>The Green House</i>, about a brothel in a Peruvian town that brings together the innocent and the corrupt; <i>The Feast of the Goat</i>, a vivid re-creation of the Dominican Republic during the final days of General Rafael Trujillo’s insidious regime; and <i>Aunt Julia and the Scriptwriter</i>, a comedic semi-autobiographical account of an aspiring writer named Marito Varguitas, who falls in love with Julia, the divorced sister-in-law of his Uncle Lucho. <br /><br />He is also a widely read and respected essayist, writing everything from newspaper opinion pieces to critical works on other writers, including <i>The Perpetual Orgy</i> on Flaubert.<br /><br />Vargas Llosa is also active outside the literary arena, and was a serious contender for the presidency of Peru in 1990 (eventually losing to the now disgraced Alberto Fujimori), an experience he documented in his memoir, <i>A Fish in the Water</i>. <br /><br />On the controversial nature of some of his work he said, “The writer’s job is to write with rigor, with commitment, to defend what they believe with all the talent they have. I think that’s part of the moral obligation of a writer, which cannot be only purely artistic. I think a writer has some kind of responsibility at least to participate in the civic debate. I think literature is impoverished, if it becomes cut from the main agenda of people, of society, of life.” <br /><br />He was awarded the Nobel Prize in Literature for the year 2010, "for his cartography of structures of power &amp; his trenchant images of the individual's resistance, revolt, and defeat".<br /><br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://us.macmillan.com/author/mariovargasllosa">http://us.macmillan.com/author/mariov...</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="15721658988519803355" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>
</div>
